</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg/20px-Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg" decoding="async" width="17" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg/40px-Aegopodium_podagraria1_ies.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="700" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Environment" title="Portal:Environment">Environment&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="mw-image-border noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Earth_Day_Flag.png/20px-Earth_Day_Flag.png" decoding="async" width="17" height="11" class="mw-file-element" href="/wiki/Portal:Ecology" title="Portal:Ecology">Ecology&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Pollution_sidebar" title="Template:Pollution sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Pollution_sidebar" title="Template talk:Pollution sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Pollution_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Pollution sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Milan_skyscrapers_aerial_view.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milan_skyscrapers_aerial_view.jpg/330px-Milan_skyscrapers_aerial_view.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="224" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Milan_skyscrapers_aerial_view.jpg/960px-Milan_skyscrapers_aerial_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2693" data-file-height="2008" /></a><figcaption>Example of dense urban living without green spaces which leads to a pronounced urban heat island effect (<a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, Italy)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:New_York_City-Manhattan-Central_Park_(Gentry).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/New_York_City-Manhattan-Central_Park_%28Gentry%29.jpg/330px-New_York_City-Manhattan-Central_Park_%28Gentry%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/New_York_City-Manhattan-Central_Park_%28Gentry%29.jpg/960px-New_York_City-Manhattan-Central_Park_%28Gentry%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1100" /></a><figcaption>Example of an inner city green space, which can reduce the urban heat island effect (<a href="/wiki/Central_Park,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Park, New York">Central Park, New York</a>)</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <b>urban heat island</b> (<b>UHI</b>) effect is a <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">climatological</a> phenomenon in which <a href="/wiki/Urban_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban areas">urban areas</a> experience significantly warmer temperatures than surrounding <a href="/wiki/Rural_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Rural areas">rural areas</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> difference is usually larger at night than during the day,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Phelan&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phelan-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is most apparent when <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a> are weak, under <a href="/wiki/Block_(meteorology)" title="Block (meteorology)">block</a> conditions, noticeably during the <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>.
The main cause of the UHI effect is from the modification of land surfaces, while <a href="/wiki/Waste_heat" title="Waste heat">waste heat</a> generated by energy usage is a secondary contributor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dx.doi.org&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dx.doi.org-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-liyan2012&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liyan2012-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Urban areas occupy about 0.5% of the Earth's land surface but host more than half of the world's population.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a population center grows, it tends to expand its area and increase its average temperature. The term <b>heat island</b> is also used; the term can be used to refer to any area that is relatively hotter than the surrounding, but generally refers to human-disturbed areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Monthly <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">rainfall</a> is greater downwind of cities, partially due to the UHI. Increases in heat within urban centers increases the length of <a href="/wiki/Growing_season" title="Growing season">growing seasons</a>, decreases <a href="/wiki/Air_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Air quality">air quality</a> by increasing the production of pollutants such as <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, and decreases water quality as warmer waters flow into area streams and put stress on their <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>.
</p><p>Not all cities have a distinct urban heat island, and the heat island characteristics depend strongly on the background climate of the area where the <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">city</a> is located.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SUE&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SUE-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The impact in a city can significantly change based on its local environment. Heat can be reduced by <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">tree cover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_green_space" title="Urban green space">green space</a>, which act as sources of shade and promote <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evaporative</a> cooling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Waldrop&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waldrop-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other options include <a href="/wiki/Green_roof" title="Green roof">green roofs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passive_daytime_radiative_cooling" title="Passive daytime radiative cooling">passive daytime radiative cooling</a> applications, ventilation corridors, the <a href="/wiki/Reflective_surfaces_(climate_engineering)" title="Reflective surfaces (climate engineering)">use of lighter-colored surfaces</a>, and less absorptive building materials. These reflect more <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> and absorb less heat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:13&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is not the cause of urban heat islands, but it is causing more frequent and more intense <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heat waves</a>, which in turn amplify the urban heat island effect in cities (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033199720">.mw-parser-output div.crossreference{padding-left:0}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable crossreference">see <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_cities" title="Climate change and cities">climate change and cities</a></span>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 993">&#58;&#8202;993&#8202;</span></sup> Compact and dense <a href="/wiki/Urban_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban Development">urban development</a> may also increase the urban heat island effect, leading to higher temperatures and increased <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_index" title="Ultraviolet index">exposure</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definition">Definition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A definition of <i>urban heat island</i> is: "The relative warmth of a city compared with surrounding rural areas."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2926">&#58;&#8202;2926&#8202;</span></sup> This relative warmth is caused by "heat trapping due to land use, the configuration and design of the <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a>, including street layout and building size, the heat-absorbing properties of urban building materials, reduced ventilation, reduced greenery and water features, and domestic and industrial heat emissions generated directly from human activities".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2926">&#58;&#8202;2926&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Description">Description</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Urban_heat_island.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Urban_heat_island.svg/500px-Urban_heat_island.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="195" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Urban_heat_island.svg/960px-Urban_heat_island.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="382" /></a><figcaption>Mechanism of the urban heat island effect: the densely-built <a href="/wiki/Downtown" title="Downtown">downtown</a> areas tend to be warmer than suburban residential areas or rural areas. </figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HeatIsland_Kanto_en.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/HeatIsland_Kanto_en.png/500px-HeatIsland_Kanto_en.png" decoding="async" width="390" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/HeatIsland_Kanto_en.png/960px-HeatIsland_Kanto_en.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, an example of an urban heat island. Normal temperatures of Tokyo go up higher than those of the surrounding area.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Diurnal_variability">Diurnal variability</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diurnal variability"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Urban_heat_island_variation.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Urban_heat_island_variation.jpg/500px-Urban_heat_island_variation.jpg" decoding="async" width="390" height="223" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Urban_heat_island_variation.jpg/960px-Urban_heat_island_variation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3799" data-file-height="2171" /></a><figcaption>Cities often experience stronger urban heat island effects at night; effects can vary with location and topography of metropolitan areas.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Throughout the daytime, particularly when the skies are cloudless, urban surfaces are warmed by the absorption of <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>. Surfaces in the urban areas tend to warm faster than those of the surrounding rural areas. By virtue of their high <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacities</a>, urban surfaces act as a reservoir of heat energy. For example, concrete can hold roughly 2,000 times as much heat as an equivalent volume of air.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "This study shows that urban surfaces such as concrete absorb and store large amounts of heat during the day, confirming the high heat capacity effect described in urban heat islands." As a result, high daytime surface temperatures within the UHI can be easily seen via thermal <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a>. As is often the case with daytime heating, this warming also has the effect of generating <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convective</a> winds within the urban <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundary_layer" title="Planetary boundary layer">boundary layer</a>. At night, the situation reverses. The absence of solar heating leads to the decrease of atmospheric convection and the stabilization of urban boundary layer. If enough stabilization occurs, an <a href="/wiki/Inversion_(meteorology)" title="Inversion (meteorology)">inversion layer</a> is formed. This traps urban air near the surface, keeping surface air warm from the still-warm urban surfaces, resulting in warmer nighttime air temperatures within the UHI.
</p><p>Generally speaking, the difference in temperature between the urban and surrounding rural area is more pronounced at night than in daytime.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, in the United States, the temperature in urban areas tends to be warmer than the surrounding area by about 1–7&#160;°F (0.55–3.9&#160;°C) during the daytime, and about  2–5&#160;°F (1.1–2.8&#160;°C) warmer at night.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the difference is more pronounced during the day in arid climates such as those in southeastern China and Taiwan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have shown that diurnal variability is impacted by several factors including local climate and weather, seasonality, humidity, vegetation, surfaces, and materials in the built environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:22&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Seasonal_variability">Seasonal variability</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Seasonal variability"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Seasonal variability is less well understood than diurnal variability of the urban heat island temperature difference.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Complex relationships between precipitation, vegetation, solar radiation, and surface materials in various local climate zones play interlocking roles that influence seasonal patterns of temperature variation in a particular urban heat island.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:8&#95;23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Measurements_and_predictions">Measurements and predictions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Measurements and predictions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Urban_Heat_Island_Index_(UHII)"><span id="Urban_Heat_Island_Index_.28UHII.29"></span>Urban Heat Island Index (UHII)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Urban Heat Island Index (UHII)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One method to quantify the UHI effect within urban areas is the UHI Index created by the <a href="/wiki/California_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="California Environmental Protection Agency">Californian EPA</a> in 2015. It compares the temperature of a surveyed area and rural reference points upwind from the surveyed area, at a height of two meters above ground level. The difference in temperature in degrees <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a> is taken hourly and differences with an increased urban temperature compared to the reference points are summed up, creating a number of degree-Celsius-hours, which is the UHI Index of the surveyed area. The measure of Celsius-hours might be averaged over many days, but is specified as Celsius-hours per averaged day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The index was created to estimate the expected use of air conditioning and resulting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> in California.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The index does not consider values of or differences in wind-speed, humidity, or <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">solar influx</a>, which might influence <a href="/wiki/Apparent_temperature" title="Apparent temperature">perceived temperature</a> or the operation of air conditioners.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:7&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Models_and_simulations">Models and simulations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Models and simulations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>If a city or <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">town</a> has a good system of taking weather observations the UHI can be measured directly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An alternative is to use a complex simulation of the location to calculate the UHI, or to use an approximate empirical method.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such models allow the UHI to be included in estimates of future temperatures rises within cities due to climate change.
</p><p>Leonard O. Myrup published the first comprehensive numerical treatment to predict the effects of the urban heat island (UHI) in 1969.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The heat island effect was found to be the net result of several competing physical processes. In general, reduced evaporation in the city center and the thermal properties of the city building and paving materials are the dominant parameters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Modern simulation environments include <a href="/wiki/ENVI-met" title="ENVI-met">ENVI-met</a>, which simulates all interactions between building and ground surfaces, plants and ambient air.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Causes">Causes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Causes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:High-rise_buildings_of_Manhattan_during_sunset.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/High-rise_buildings_of_Manhattan_during_sunset.jpg/250px-High-rise_buildings_of_Manhattan_during_sunset.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/High-rise_buildings_of_Manhattan_during_sunset.jpg/500px-High-rise_buildings_of_Manhattan_during_sunset.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a><figcaption>High-rise buildings of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, an example of dense urban living</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Newyork_heat_island.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Newyork_heat_island.jpg/250px-Newyork_heat_island.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="502" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Newyork_heat_island.jpg/500px-Newyork_heat_island.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="1084" /></a><figcaption>Thermal (top) and vegetation (bottom) locations around <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> via infrared satellite imagery. A comparison of the images shows that where vegetation is dense, temperatures are lower.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Heatwave#Formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heatwave">Heatwave §&#160;Formation</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Urban_design">Urban design</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Urban design"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Urban_design" title="Urban design">Urban design</a></div>
<p>There are several causes of an urban heat island (UHI) related to common <a href="/wiki/Urban_design" title="Urban design">urban design</a> aspects. For example, dark surfaces absorb significantly more <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>, which causes urban concentrations of roads and buildings to heat more than suburban and rural areas during the day;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dx.doi.org&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dx.doi.org-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> materials commonly used in urban areas for pavement and roofs, such as <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asphalt_concrete" title="Asphalt concrete">asphalt</a>, have significantly different thermal bulk properties (including <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">heat capacity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>) and surface radiative properties (<a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emissivity" title="Emissivity">emissivity</a>) than the surrounding rural areas. This causes a change in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_budget" title="Earth&#39;s energy budget">energy budget</a> of the urban area, often leading to higher temperatures than surrounding rural areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Okeljnljn&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okeljnljn-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pavers_(flooring)" title="Pavers (flooring)">Pavements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parking_lot" title="Parking lot">parking lots</a>, roads or, more generally speaking <a href="/wiki/Transport_infrastructure" class="mw-redirect" title="Transport infrastructure">transport infrastructure</a>, contribute significantly to the urban heat island effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, pavement infrastructure is a main contributor to urban heat during summer afternoons in <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a>, United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another major reason is the lack of <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a> (for example, through lack of vegetation) in urban areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The U.S. Forest Service found in 2018 that cities in the United States are losing 36 million trees each year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With a decreased amount of vegetation, cities also lose the shade and evaporative cooling effect of trees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other causes of a UHI are due to geometric effects. The tall buildings within many urban areas provide multiple surfaces for the reflection and absorption of sunlight, increasing the efficiency with which urban areas are heated. This is called the "<a href="/wiki/Urban_canyon" title="Urban canyon">urban canyon effect</a>". Another effect of buildings is the blocking of wind, which also inhibits cooling by <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> and prevents pollutants from dissipating. Waste heat from automobiles, air conditioning, industry, and other sources also contributes to the UHI.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-liyan2012&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liyan2012-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Heat islands can be affected by proximity to different types of land cover, so that proximity to barren land causes urban land to become hotter and proximity to vegetation makes it cooler.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Poor_landscaping">Poor landscaping</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Poor landscaping"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The design of personal landscapes like yards and public areas like city parks can have a large effect on UHIs.  A well designed landscape built around native plants can survive with natural rainfall and will be resilient to heat waves and excessive heat from parking lots.  These landscapes provide shade, stability, and moisture to the surrounding areas.  Lawns of non-native grasses will not be resilient against changes in climate, temperature, rainfall, or water availability.  Large unbroken parking lots can heat up much hotter than surrounding areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Factoring in space for tree cover, shade structures, and resilient plants can prevent a development from becoming dangerously hot or contributing to UHIs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Air pollution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>High levels of <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> in urban areas can also increase the UHI, as many forms of pollution change the radiative properties of the atmosphere.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Okeljnljn&#95;35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okeljnljn-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UHI not only raises urban temperatures but also increases ozone concentrations because ozone is a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> whose formation will accelerate with the increase of temperature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Climate_change_as_an_amplifier">Climate change as an amplifier</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate change as an amplifier"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_cities" title="Climate change and cities">Climate change and cities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation#Heatwaves" title="Climate change adaptation">Climate change adaptation §&#160;Heatwaves</a></div>
<p>Climate change is not a cause but an amplifier of the urban heat island effect. The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Sixth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Sixth Assessment Report">IPCC Sixth Assessment Report</a> from 2022 summarized the available research accordingly: "Climate change increases <a href="/wiki/Heat_stress" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat stress">heat stress</a> risks in cities [...] and amplifies the urban heat island across Asian cities at 1.5&#160;°C and 2&#160;°C warming levels, both substantially larger than under present climates [...]."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 66">&#58;&#8202;66&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>The report goes on to say: "In a warming world, increasing air temperature makes the urban heat island effect in cities worse. One key risk is <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heatwaves</a> in cities that are likely to affect half of the future global urban population, with negative impacts on human health and economic productivity."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 993">&#58;&#8202;993&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>There are unhelpful interactions between heat and built infrastructure: These interactions increase the risk of heat stress for people living in cities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 993">&#58;&#8202;993&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Increased_urbanization">Increased urbanization</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Increased urbanization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Increased urbanization is a major leading cause for increased urban heat risks, as it replaces vegetated areas with impervious surfaces and concentrates people into smaller land areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2a&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2a-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  There are measurable and designable factors that determine if a urban or suburban area will have a heat island effect.  Suburban areas usually have more greenery but can be subject to rapid heating if drought conditions result in there not being enough water for private <a href="/wiki/Lawn" title="Lawn">lawns</a>.  Suburban areas also sprawl-out faster and if <a href="/wiki/Car_dependency" title="Car dependency">car-dependant</a>, require large amounts of asphalt and large parking lots which heat up quick and retain heat.  Cities are more likely to have UHIs but can be designed to have tall towers surrounded by large parks which can exist harmoniously with the surrounding climate.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Spatial_distribution">Spatial distribution</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Spatial distribution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Historic urbanization processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Redlining" title="Redlining">redlining</a> in the United States, have lasting impacts on land use  and may perpetuate disparities in urban heat through unequal distribution of vegetation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3a&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3a-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Access to healthcare, public transportation, adequate housing, and reliable energy typically govern disparities to heat, of which many of these communities may lack access to.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3a&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3a-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such differences in spatial distribution leave certain, localized neighborhood at risk for micro urban heat islands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4a&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4a-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Socially vulnerable groups are known to live in more densely populated areas with little vegetation that in turn have higher threats to heat exposure and often have adapting to and mitigation their exposure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4a&#95;51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4a-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Housing_conditions">Housing conditions</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Housing conditions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Housing conditions are a factor that contributes to urban heat inequity. Living on the top-floor, having a home with a dark roof, and poor insulation exacerbate heating conditions during hot weather.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Lower income individuals may also not have access to air-conditioning or be unable to afford the increased electricity usage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In condensed cities, opening the windows for airflow and cooler air could introduce air pollution and odor into the home.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> High-density housing is Asia often has constraints on building updates that promote cooling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Space_poverty">Space poverty</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Space poverty"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Another contributor to inequality is space poverty, which refers to a significantly reduced indoor residential space in a home.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Space poverty is a significant issue in Hong Kong, where low-income individuals live in extremely small, sub-divided units, which has led to overcrowding and indoor air pollution.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The units promote little movement, and all furniture, kitchen appliances, and bathroom necessities must fit into one sub-divided room.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These units are typically developed from derelict buildings that have poor ventilation and less access to proper space cooling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> High-density housing is Asia often has constraints on building updates that promote cooling, and residents often lack the space and authority to complete any improvements to their condition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These residents typically seek out adequate <a href="/wiki/Third_place" title="Third place">third places</a>, such as libraries, parks, and cafés, to access proper cooling and space.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Impacts">Impacts</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Impacts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dubai_skyline_2015_(crop).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dubai_skyline_2015_%28crop%29.jpg/250px-Dubai_skyline_2015_%28crop%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dubai_skyline_2015_%28crop%29.jpg/500px-Dubai_skyline_2015_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1558" data-file-height="1179" /></a><figcaption>Example of urbanization: <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="On_weather_and_climate">On weather and climate</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: On weather and climate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Aside from the effect on temperature, UHIs can produce secondary effects on local meteorology, including the altering of local wind patterns, the development of <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, and the rates of precipitation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The extra heat provided by the UHI leads to greater upward motion, which can induce additional shower and thunderstorm activity. In addition, the UHI creates during the day a local low pressure area where relatively moist air from its rural surroundings converges, possibly leading to more favorable conditions for cloud formation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hee&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hee-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rainfall rates downwind of cities are increased between 48% and 116%. Partly as a result of this warming, monthly rainfall is about 28% greater between 20 and 40 miles (32 and 64&#160;km) downwind of cities, compared with upwind.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some cities show a total precipitation increase of 51%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One study concluded that cities change the climate in area two–four times larger than their own area.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One 1999 comparison between urban and rural areas proposed that urban heat island effects have little influence on <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Instrumental temperature record">global mean temperature trends</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others suggested that urban heat islands affect global climate by impacting the jet stream.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="On_human_health">On human health</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: On human health"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1305433154">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output
