<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Heat_illness" title="Heat illness">Heat illness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_human_health#Higher_global_temperatures_and_heat_waves_(direct_risk)" title="Effects of climate change on human health">Effects of climate change on human health §&#160;Higher global temperatures and heat waves (direct risk)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlanta_thermal.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Atlanta_thermal.jpg/330px-Atlanta_thermal.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="232" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Atlanta_thermal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="379" /></a><figcaption>Image of <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta, Georgia</a>, showing temperature distribution, with blue showing cool temperatures, red warm, and hot areas appear white</figcaption></figure>
<p>UHIs have the potential to directly influence the health and welfare of urban residents. As UHIs are characterized by increased temperature, they can potentially increase the magnitude and duration of heat waves within cities. The number of individuals exposed to extreme temperatures is increased by the UHI-induced warming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The nighttime effect of UHIs can be particularly harmful during a heat wave, as it deprives urban residents of the cool relief found in rural areas during the night.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Clarke&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Exposure to extreme urban heat can increase the risk of heat-related illnesses and mortality, particularly among elderly populations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Increased temperatures have been reported to cause <a href="/wiki/Heat_illness" title="Heat illness">heat illnesses</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Heat_stroke" title="Heat stroke">heat stroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_exhaustion" title="Heat exhaustion">heat exhaustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_syncope" title="Heat syncope">heat syncope</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heat_cramps" title="Heat cramps">heat cramps</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Review1&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review1-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Extreme heat is the deadliest form of weather in the U.S. Per a study by Professor Terri Adams-Fuller, heat waves kill more people in the U.S. than hurricanes, floods, and tornadoes combined.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These heat illnesses are more common within medium-to-large metro areas than the rest of the U.S., largely in part due to UHIs. Heat illnesses can also be compounded when combined with air pollution which is common in many urban areas.
</p><p>Heat exposure can have adverse effects on mental health. Increases in temperature can contribute to increased aggression, as well as more cases of domestic violence and substance abuse.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Greater heat can also negatively impact school performance and education. According to a study by Hyunkuk Cho of Yeungnam University, an increased number of days with extreme heat each year correlates to a decrease in student test scores.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>High UHI intensity correlates with increased concentrations of air pollutants that gathered at night, which can affect the next day's <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air quality</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DOE&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These pollutants include <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxides" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogen oxides">nitrogen oxides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Particulates" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulates">particulate matter</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHO&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The production of these pollutants combined with the higher temperatures in UHIs can quicken the production of <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DOE&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ozone at surface level is considered to be a harmful pollutant.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DOE&#95;67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies suggest that increased temperatures in UHIs can increase polluted days but also note that other factors (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">air pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cover" title="Cloud cover">cloud cover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_speed" title="Wind speed">wind speed</a>) can also have an effect on pollution.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DOE&#95;67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Studies from <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> have found that areas of the city with poorer outdoor urban air ventilation tended to have stronger urban heat island effects<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and had significantly higher all-cause mortality<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> compared to areas with better ventilation. Another study employing advanced statistical methods in Babol city, Iran, revealed a significant increase in Surface Urban Heat Island Intensity (SUHII) from 1985 to 2017, influenced by both geographic direction and time. This research, enhancing the understanding of SUHII's spatial and temporal variations, emphasizes the need for precise urban planning to mitigate the health impacts of urban heat islands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Surface UHI's are more prominent during the day and are measured using the land surface temperature and remote sensing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Heat stress vulnerability refers to an individual's predisposition, typically due to demographic factors, to be negatively impacted by heat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The measurement takes into account daily routines, location, sensitivity due to demographic factors, including age.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some demographic groups known to have higher heat stress vulnerability include the elderly, women, children, low-income households, and those who are chronically ill.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Elderly populations are included because they are often unable to regular their body temperatures and typically have pre-existing medical conditions that make them more susceptible to heat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Children are also sensitive to increased heat, which may cause stress on their developing bodies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Intensified indoor heat causes stress on the mind and body and strains domestic relations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="On_water_bodies_and_aquatic_organisms">On water bodies and aquatic organisms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: On water bodies and aquatic organisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>UHIs also impair <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a>. Hot pavement and rooftop surfaces transfer their excess heat to stormwater, which then drains into storm sewers and raises water temperatures as it is released into streams, rivers, ponds, and lakes. Additionally, increased urban water body temperatures lead to a <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">decrease in biodiversity</a> in the water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, in August 2001, rains over <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Rapids,_Iowa" title="Cedar Rapids, Iowa">Cedar Rapids, Iowa</a> led to a 10.5&#160;°C (18.9&#160;°F) rise in the nearby stream within one hour, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Fish_kill" title="Fish kill">fish kill</a> which affected an estimated 188 fish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since the temperature of the rain was comparatively cool, the deaths could be attributed to the hot pavement of the city. Similar events have been documented across the American Midwest, as well as Oregon and California.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rapid temperature changes can be stressful to aquatic ecosystems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With the temperature of the nearby buildings sometimes reaching a difference of over 50&#160;°F (28&#160;°C) from the near-surface air temperature, precipitation warms rapidly, and run-off into nearby streams, lakes and rivers (or other bodies of water) to provide excessive <a href="/wiki/Thermal_pollution" title="Thermal pollution">thermal pollution</a>. The increase in thermal pollution has the potential to increase water temperature by 20 to 30&#160;°F (11 to 17&#160;°C). This increase causes the fish species inhabiting the body of water to undergo thermal stress and shock due to the rapid change in temperature of their habitat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Permeable_paving" title="Permeable paving">Permeable pavements</a> may reduce these effects by <a href="/wiki/Percolation" title="Percolation">percolating</a> water through the pavement into subsurface storage areas where it can be dissipated through absorption and evaporation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-coolpave&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coolpave-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>UHIs can both worsen and be worsened by <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2026)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="On_animals">On animals</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: On animals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Species that are good at colonizing can use conditions provided by urban heat islands to thrive in regions outside of their normal range. Examples of this include the <a href="/wiki/Grey-headed_flying_fox" title="Grey-headed flying fox">grey-headed flying fox</a> (<i>Pteropus poliocephalus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Common_house_gecko" title="Common house gecko">common house gecko</a> (<i>Hemidactylus frenatus</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Paige&#95;S&#95;2005&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paige_S_2005-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Grey-headed flying foxes, found in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Melbourne, Australia">Melbourne, Australia</a>, colonized urban habitats following the increase in temperatures there. Increased temperatures, causing warmer winter conditions, made the city more similar in climate to the more northerly wildland habitat of the species.
</p><p>With temperate climates, urban heat islands will extend the growing season, therefore altering breeding strategies of inhabiting species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Paige&#95;S&#95;2005&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paige_S_2005-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This can be best observed in the effects that urban heat islands have on water temperature (see <a href="#on_water_bodies_and_aquatic_organisms">effects on water bodies</a>).
</p><p>Urban heat islands caused by cities have altered the <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Paige&#95;S&#95;2005&#95;79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paige_S_2005-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Selective pressures like temporal variation in food, predation and water are relaxed causing a new set of selective forces to roll out. For example, within urban habitats, insects are more abundant than in rural areas. Insects are <a href="/wiki/Ectotherms" class="mw-redirect" title="Ectotherms">ectotherms</a>. This means that they depend on the temperature of the environment to control their body temperature, making the warmer climates of the city perfect for their ability to thrive. A study done in <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a> conducted on <i><a href="/wiki/Parthenolecanium_quercifex" title="Parthenolecanium quercifex">Parthenolecanium quercifex</a></i> (oak scales), showed that this particular species preferred warmer climates and were therefore found in higher abundance in urban habitats than on <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> trees in rural habitats. Over time spent living in urban habitats, they have adapted to thrive in warmer climates than in cooler ones.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="On_energy_usage_for_cooling">On energy usage for cooling</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: On energy usage for cooling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_SLC_Urban_Heat_Island_Effect_Roof.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/NASA_SLC_Urban_Heat_Island_Effect_Roof.jpg/500px-NASA_SLC_Urban_Heat_Island_Effect_Roof.jpg" decoding="async" width="390" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/NASA_SLC_Urban_Heat_Island_Effect_Roof.jpg/960px-NASA_SLC_Urban_Heat_Island_Effect_Roof.jpg 2x" data-file-width="974" data-file-height="482" /></a><figcaption>Images of <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a> show positive correlation between white reflective roofs and cooler temperatures. Image A depicts an aerial view of Salt Lake City, Utah, site of 865000 sqft white reflective roof. Image B is a thermal infrared image of same area, showing hot (red and yellow) and cool (green and blue) spots. The reflective vinyl roof, not absorbing solar radiation, is shown in blue surrounded by other hot spots.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Another consequence of urban heat islands is the increased energy required for <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a> in cities that are in comparatively hot climates. The heat island effect costs <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> about <a href="/wiki/US$" class="mw-redirect" title="US$">US$</a> 100 million per year in energy (in the year 2000).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Through the implementation of heat island reduction strategies, significant annual net energy savings have been calculated for northern locations such as Chicago, Salt Lake City, and Toronto.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Every year in the U.S. 15% of energy goes towards the <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> of buildings in these urban heat islands. It was reported in 1998 that "the air conditioning demand has risen 10% within the last 40 years."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rosenfeld98&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenfeld98-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Increases in air conditioning use also serve to worsen the effects of UHIs at night. While cooler nights are often a reprieve from heat waves during the day, the residual heat created by the use of air conditioning systems can lead to higher nighttime temperatures. According to a study by Professor Francisco Salamanca Palou and colleagues, this residual heat can cause nighttime increases of up to 1&#160;°C in urban areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Increased energy use from air conditioners also contributes to carbon emissions, which doubly exacerbates the effects of UHIs.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Options_for_reducing_heat_island_effects">Options for reducing heat island effects</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Options for reducing heat island effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ogr%C3%B3d_botaniczny_UMCS.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Ogr%C3%B3d_botaniczny_UMCS.png/250px-Ogr%C3%B3d_botaniczny_UMCS.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Ogr%C3%B3d_botaniczny_UMCS.png/500px-Ogr%C3%B3d_botaniczny_UMCS.png 2x" data-file-width="820" data-file-height="615" /></a><figcaption>Botanical Garden in <a href="/wiki/Lublin" title="Lublin">Lublin</a>, Poland</figcaption></figure>
<p>Strategies to improve <a href="/wiki/Urban_resilience" title="Urban resilience">urban resilience</a> by reducing excessive heat in cities include: Planting trees in cities, <a href="/wiki/Reflective_surfaces_(climate_engineering)" title="Reflective surfaces (climate engineering)">cool roofs (painted white or with reflective coating)</a> and light-coloured concrete, <a href="/wiki/Green_infrastructure" title="Green infrastructure">green infrastructure</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Green_roof" title="Green roof">green roofs</a>), <a href="/wiki/Passive_daytime_radiative_cooling" title="Passive daytime radiative cooling">passive daytime radiative cooling</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The temperature difference between urban areas and the surrounding suburban or rural areas can be as much as 5&#160;°C (9.0&#160;°F). Nearly 40 percent of that increase is due to the prevalence of dark roofs, with the remainder coming from dark-coloured pavement and the declining presence of vegetation. The heat island effect can be counteracted slightly by using white or reflective materials to build houses, roofs, pavements, and roads, thus increasing the overall albedo of the city.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Concentric expansion of cities is unfavourable in terms of the urban heat island phenomenon. It is recommended to plan the development of cities in strips, consistent with the hydrographic network, taking into account green areas with various plant species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this way, it was planned to build urban settlements stretching over large areas, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Kielce" title="Kielce">Kielce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Szczecin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gdynia" title="Gdynia">Gdynia</a> in Poland, <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> in Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kiel" title="Kiel">Kiel</a> in Germany.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Planting_trees_in_cities">Planting trees in cities</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Planting trees in cities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Urban_forest" title="Urban forest">Urban forest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_forestry" title="Urban forestry">Urban forestry</a></div>
<p>Planting trees around the city can be another way of increasing albedo and decreasing the urban heat island effect. It is recommended to plant <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> trees because they can provide many benefits such as more shade in the summer and not blocking warmth in winter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rosenfield97&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenfield97-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Trees are a necessary feature in combating most of the urban heat island effect because they reduce air temperatures by 10&#160;°F (5.6&#160;°C),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and surface temperatures by up to 20–45&#160;°F (11–25&#160;°C).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another benefit of having trees in a city is that trees also help fight global warming by absorbing CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> from the atmosphere.
</p><p>Recent syntheses show that increasing street-tree canopy measurably cools pedestrian-level air temperatures, with effects varying by climate, canopy density, and urban form. A 2024 meta-analysis across 110 cities reports typical ~1–2&#160;°C daytime cooling, and a 2024 field study found that locations with no canopy within 10 m were up to five times more likely to exceed 32.2&#160;°C, with air temperature declining linearly as canopy increased.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Li2024&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li2024-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ettinger2024&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ettinger2024-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tree species diversity and canopy coverage are associated with the cooling effects of urban green spaces, with higher diversity linked to greater temperature reductions in certain seasons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Urban green infrastructure (UGI) are integrated networks of green spaces in cities and are developed in both private and public areas of the city.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples of UGI include urban parks with trees and shrub cover, rows of trees along a street, private gardens, rooftop gardens, and other green space throughout a city.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1a&#95;52-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1a-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Properly utilized UGI promotes more equitable heat distribution, where improper usage only perpetuates heat inequity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UGI provides shading and <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>, which reduces both air and land temperatures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Therefore, a lack of UGI in marginalized communities reduces the land's temperature regulating capabilities promotes heat inequity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UGI is widely recognized as an efficient, sustainable, and non-controversial solution to heat inequity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cool_roofs_and_light-coloured_concrete">Cool roofs and light-coloured concrete</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Cool roofs and light-coloured concrete"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reflective_surfaces_(climate_engineering)" title="Reflective surfaces (climate engineering)">Reflective surfaces (climate engineering)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:20080708_Chicago_City_Hall_Green_Roof.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/20080708_Chicago_City_Hall_Green_Roof.JPG/250px-20080708_Chicago_City_Hall_Green_Roof.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/20080708_Chicago_City_Hall_Green_Roof.JPG/500px-20080708_Chicago_City_Hall_Green_Roof.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Green roof of <a href="/wiki/Chicago_City_Hall" title="Chicago City Hall">Chicago City Hall</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Painting rooftops white has become a common strategy to reduce the heat island effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated3&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In cities, there are many dark coloured surfaces that absorb the heat of the sun in turn lowering the albedo of the city.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated3&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> White rooftops allow high solar reflectance and high solar emittance, increasing the albedo of the city or area the effect is occurring.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-autogenerated3&#95;95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Green roofs and cool roofs can play a big role in helping cities manage extreme heat. In warmer regions, cool roofs were especially effective, with some cutting cooling energy use completely for certain low-rise buildings. Green roofs may be more beneficial in colder climates because they provide insulation and help reduce the need for heating during winter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, covering rooftops with a reflective coating, has shown to be an effective measure to reduce solar heat gain. A study led by Oscar Brousse from University College London, which simulated the impact of various cooling measures in London found that rooftops, which were either painted white or had reflective coating, proved to be the most effective solution for reducing outdoor temperatures at the pedestrian level, outperforming solar panels, green roofs, and tree cover. The study simulated the impact of various cooling measures in London during a 2018 heatwave, finding that the so-called cool roofs could reduce average outdoor temperatures by 1.2&#160;°C, and up to 2&#160;°C in certain areas. In comparison, additional tree cover reduced temperatures by 0.3&#160;°C and solar panels by 0.5&#160;°C.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Relative to remedying the other sources of the problem, replacing dark roofing requires the least amount of investment for the most immediate return. A <a href="/wiki/Cool_roof" class="mw-redirect" title="Cool roof">cool roof</a> made from a reflective material such as vinyl reflects at least 75 percent of the sun's rays, and emit at least 70 percent of the solar radiation absorbed by the building envelope. Asphalt built-up roofs (BUR), by comparison, reflect 6 percent to 26 percent of solar radiation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Using light-coloured concrete has proven effective in reflecting up to 50% more light than asphalt and reducing ambient temperature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-coolpave&#95;2&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coolpave_2-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A low albedo value, characteristic of black asphalt, absorbs a large percentage of solar heat creating warmer near-surface temperatures. Paving with light-coloured concrete, in addition to replacing asphalt with light-coloured concrete, communities may be able to lower average temperatures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-worldchange&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldchange-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, research into the interaction between reflective pavements and buildings has found that, unless the nearby buildings are fitted with reflective glass, solar radiation reflected off light-coloured pavements can increase building temperatures, increasing air conditioning demands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yaghoobian&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaghoobian-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are specific paint formulations for daytime radiative cooling that reflect up to 98.1% of sunlight.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Green_infrastructure">Green infrastructure</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Green infrastructure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Green_infrastructure" title="Green infrastructure">Green infrastructure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_roof" title="Green roof">Green roof</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Budapest,_CAF_Urbos_3,_4.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Grassed tramway track in Budapest, Hungary" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Budapest%2C_CAF_Urbos_3%2C_4.jpg/250px-Budapest%2C_CAF_Urbos_3%2C_4.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Budapest%2C_CAF_Urbos_3%2C_4.jpg/500px-Budapest%2C_CAF_Urbos_3%2C_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Green_track" title="Green track">Grassed tramway track</a> in Budapest, Hungary</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Green_roofs" class="mw-redirect" title="Green roofs">Green roofs</a> are excellent insulators during the warm weather months and the plants cool the surrounding environment. Plants can improve air quality as they absorb carbon dioxide and concomitantly produce oxygen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Green roofs can also have positive impacts on stormwater management and energy consumption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cost can be a barrier to implementing a green roof.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:12&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several factors influence the cost of a green roof, including design and soil depth, location, and the price of labor and equipment in that market, which is typically lower in more developed markets where there is more experience designing and installing green roofs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The individualized context of each green roof presents a challenge for making broad comparisons and assessments, and focusing only on monetary costs may leave out the social, environmental, and public health benefits green roofs provide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:12&#95;107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Global comparisons of green roof performance are further challenged by the lack of a shared framework for making such comparisons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:12&#95;107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Stormwater management is another option to help mitigate the effect of the urban heat island. Stormwater management is the controlling the water produced by the storm in a way that protects property and infrastructure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Urban infrastructure like streets, sidewalks, and parking lots do not allow for water to penetrate into the earth's surface causing water to flood. By using stormwater management, you can control the flow of the water in ways that can mitigate UHI effect. One way is using a stormwater management technique called pervious pavement system (PPS). This technique has been used in over 30 countries and found to be successful in stormwater management and UHI mitigation. The PPS allows water to flow through the pavement allowing for the water to be absorbed causing the area to be cooled by evaporation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Green_parking_lot" title="Green parking lot">Green parking lots</a> use vegetation and surfaces other than asphalt to limit the urban heat island effect.
</p>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Green_infrastructure" title="Green infrastructure">Green infrastructure</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Green_infrastructure&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Meridian_Hill_Bioswale.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Meridian_Hill_Bioswale.jpg/250px-Meridian_Hill_Bioswale.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Meridian_Hill_Bioswale.jpg/500px-Meridian_Hill_Bioswale.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a><figcaption>Runoff from the vicinity flows into an adjacent <a href="/wiki/Bioswale" title="Bioswale">bioswale</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Green_infrastructure" title="Green infrastructure">Green infrastructure</a> or blue-green infrastructure refers to a network that provides the "ingredients" for solving urban and climatic challenges by building with nature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Green&#95;infrastructure&#95;Urban&#95;green-blue&#95;grids&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_infrastructure_Urban_green-blue_grids-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The main components of this approach include <a href="/wiki/Stormwater" title="Stormwater">stormwater</a> management, <a href="/wiki/Climate_adaptation" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate adaptation">climate adaptation</a>, the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Heat_stress" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat stress">heat stress</a>, increasing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Food production">food production</a>, better <a href="/wiki/Air_quality" class="mw-redirect" title="Air quality">air quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> production, clean water, and <a href="/wiki/Soil_health" title="Soil health">healthy soils</a>, as well as more human centered functions, such as increased <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a> through recreation and the provision of shade and shelter in and around towns and cities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Green infrastructure also serves to provide an ecological framework for social, economic, and environmental health of the surroundings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Green&#95;infrastructure&#95;issuu.com&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_infrastructure_issuu.com-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More recently scholars and activists have also called for green infrastructure that promotes social inclusion and equity rather than reinforcing pre-existing structures of unequal access to nature-based services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Passive_daytime_radiative_cooling">Passive daytime radiative cooling</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Passive daytime radiative cooling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Passive_daytime_radiative_cooling" title="Passive daytime radiative cooling">Passive daytime radiative cooling</a></div>
<p>A passive daytime radiative cooling roof application can double the energy savings of a white roof, attributed to high solar reflectance and thermal emittance in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared_window" title="Infrared window">infrared window</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:352&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:352-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with the highest cooling potential in hot and dry cities such as <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">Las Vegas</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:36&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:36-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When installed on roofs in dense urban areas, passive daytime radiative cooling panels can significantly lower outdoor surface temperatures at the pedestrian level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:13&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Society and culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="History_of_research">History of research</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: History of research"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The phenomenon was first investigated and described by <a href="/wiki/Luke_Howard_(meteorologist)" title="Luke Howard (meteorologist)">Luke Howard</a> in the 1810s, although he was not the one to name the phenomenon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A description of the very first report of the UHI by Luke Howard said that the urban center of London was warmer at night than the surrounding countryside by 2.1&#160;°C (3.7&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Investigations of the urban atmosphere continued throughout the nineteenth century. Between the 1920s and the 1940s, researchers in the emerging field of local climatology or <a href="/wiki/Microscale_meteorology" title="Microscale meteorology">microscale meteorology</a> in Europe, Mexico, India, Japan, and the United States pursued new methods to understand the phenomenon.
</p><p>In 1929, Albert Peppler used the term in a German publication believed to be the first instance of an equivalent to urban heat island: <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">städtische Wärmeinsel</i></span> (which is <i>urban heat island</i> in German).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Between 1990 and 2000, about 30 studies were published annually; by 2010, that number had increased to 100, and by 2015, it was more than 300.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Leonard O. Myrup published the first comprehensive numerical treatment to predict the effects of the urban heat island (UHI) in 1969.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His paper surveys UHI and criticizes then-existing theories as being excessively qualitative.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Aspects_of_social_inequality">Aspects of social inequality</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Aspects of social inequality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>Urban heat inequity</b>, also termed thermal inequity, is an unequal distribution of heat in urban areas or neighborhoods within them, which causes disproportionate impacts to people living in those communities. Unequal threat of heat stress in urban environments is often correlated with differences in demographics, including racial and ethnic background, income, education level, and age.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the general impacts of urban heat inequity depend on the city studied, negative effects typically act on historically marginalized communities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;49-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The idea is closely tied to the <a href="/wiki/Heat_island_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat island effect">urban heat island effect</a>, where a major cause to urban heat inequity is increased urbanization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2a&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2a-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some studies suggest that the effects of UHIs on health may be disproportionate, since the impacts may be unevenly distributed based on a variety of factors such as age,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHO&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diaz&#95;article&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diaz_article-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> ethnicity and socioeconomic status.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Harlan&#95;article&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harlan_article-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This raises the possibility of health impacts from UHIs being an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a> issue. Studies have shown that <a href="/wiki/Communities_of_color" class="mw-redirect" title="Communities of color">communities of color</a> in the United States have been disproportionately affected by UHI.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is a correlation between neighborhood income and tree canopy cover.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Demand&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demand-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Low-income neighborhoods tend to have significantly fewer trees than neighborhoods with higher incomes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Income&#95;Inequality&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Income_Inequality-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers hypothesized that less-well-off neighborhoods do not have the financial resources to plant and maintain trees. <a href="/wiki/Affluent" class="mw-redirect" title="Affluent">Affluent</a> neighborhoods can afford more trees, on "both public and private property".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One reason for this discrepancy is that wealthier homeowners and communities can afford more land, which can be kept open as <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green space</a>, whereas poorer housing often takes the form of <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">rentals</a>, where landowners try to maximize their <a href="/wiki/Profit_(economics)" title="Profit (economics)">profit</a> by putting as much housing <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">density</a> as possible on their land.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On a global scale, there are discrepancies in the effects of UHIs in different regions of the world. While overall heat exposure is increasing worldwide, its effects have increased faster in the Global South in recent decades, per a study by Professor Kanging Huang and colleagues.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The disproportionate impact of UHIs on the Global South exacerbates already occurring environmental injustices. Because many countries in the equator are naturally hot and humid, these areas are particularly susceptible to the effects of UHIs. One World Bank study has found a 7.0° disparity between the hottest and coolest neighborhoods in Bandung, Indonesia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:We_Parked_Our_Car_In_This_Worlds_Largest_Parking_Lot,_Between_16Th_And_26Th_Sts._On_Columbus_Drive_(NBY_417663).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/We_Parked_Our_Car_In_This_Worlds_Largest_Parking_Lot%2C_Between_16Th_And_26Th_Sts._On_Columbus_Drive_%28NBY_417663%29.jpg/250px-We_Parked_Our_Car_In_This_Worlds_Largest_Parking_Lot%2C_Between_16Th_And_26Th_Sts._On_Columbus_Drive_%28NBY_417663%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/We_Parked_Our_Car_In_This_Worlds_Largest_Parking_Lot%2C_Between_16Th_And_26Th_Sts._On_Columbus_Drive_%28NBY_417663%29.jpg/500px-We_Parked_Our_Car_In_This_Worlds_Largest_Parking_Lot%2C_Between_16Th_And_26Th_Sts._On_Columbus_Drive_%28NBY_417663%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="806" data-file-height="507" /></a><figcaption>Commemorative postcard from the worlds largest parking lot in the 1930's, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_US_experience">The US experience</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The US experience"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There is a correlation in the U.S. between ethnicity and exposure to UHIs. Within most U.S. cities, people of color are more likely to live in areas of high Surface Urban Heat Island Intensity than white people in the same cities. According to a study by climatologist Angel Hsu and colleagues, "the average person of color lives in a <a href="/wiki/Census_tract" title="Census tract">census tract</a> with higher SUHI intensity than non-Hispanic whites in all but 6 of the 175 largest urbanized areas" in the U.S.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6a&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6a-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2023 policy brief further found that historically redlined and low‑income Black neighborhoods—already burdened by urban heat—also experience clusters of violent crime, amplifying intersecting vulnerabilities in these communities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Economic status also plays a role in the human effects on UHIs. Not only are people of lower economic classes more likely to live in UHIs, but they are also less likely to be able to afford commodities such as air conditioning. Like the correlation between SUHI intensity and ethnicity, a similar pattern can be seen when comparing households under the poverty line against those with an income more than double the poverty line.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6a&#95;134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6a-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>UHIs can have particularly strong effects on African Americans with chronic diseases. African Americans have higher rates of various chronic diseases, such as asthma and diabetes, than the general population. Per Professor Pamela Jackson and colleagues, these diseases can be exacerbated by extreme heat, leading to health problems such as hypertension or stroke.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have also noted that the spread of <a href="/wiki/Impervious_surface" title="Impervious surface">impervious surfaces</a>, such as concrete, tar, and asphalt, is correlated with neighborhoods of low socioeconomic status across various U.S. cities and states.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The presence of these materials serves as a predictor of "intra-urban variation in temperature".
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Redlining,_Urban_Heat_Exposure,_and_Health_Inequity_in_U.S._cities"><span id="Redlining.2C_Urban_Heat_Exposure.2C_and_Health_Inequity_in_U.S._cities"></span>Redlining, Urban Heat Exposure, and Health Inequity in U.S. cities</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Redlining, Urban Heat Exposure, and Health Inequity in U.S. cities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Redlining worked as a housing policy created and put to use in the 1930s, under the Roosevelt Administration, by the Home Owners' Loan Corporation (HOLC). This policy, when implemented, categorized neighborhoods by perceived investment risk. Notably, areas with higher populations of minorities and low-income residents were discriminated against using this policy, often labeled as hazardous and outlined in red on HOLC maps. This significantly restricted not only access to housing loans but also led to long-term divestment from these communities.
</p><p>A study analyzing 108 U.S. cities found that formerly redlined neighborhoods are, on average, 2.6&#160;°C (4.7&#160;°F) hotter than non-redlined areas, and as the authors noted, this was largely due to a lack of tree canopy and the increased presence of heat-absorbent surfaces like asphalt and other dark clustered infrastructure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In cities like Durham, Fresno, and Pittsburgh, "D" graded areas have substantially lower tree coverage compared to wealthier, "A" graded neighborhoods, which are also predominantly white.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similarly, Phoenix, Arizona, a rapidly warming city, also shows high surface temperatures in formerly redlined communities reaching up to 10-15&#160;°F (5.6–8.3&#160;°C) higher than other neighborhoods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to higher temperatures, these neighborhoods also experience higher levels of air pollution due to their proximity to highways and industrial zones, a characterization of previously redlined areas. Cities like Los Angeles experienced freeway construction in the 1950s that routed interstates through redlined areas, including neighborhoods like Boyle Heights and South Los Angeles. These areas have since experienced much higher concentrations of vehicular emissions such as diesel exhaust and fine particulate matter (PM2.5), which are also closely associated with higher risks of respiratory illness, cardiovascular disease, and birth defects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On an international scale, research has also suggested that urban air pollution, like that of vehicular emissions, may directly intensify the urban heat island effect. In China, an urban haze effect, essentially a miniature greenhouse layer which prevents outgoing thermal radiation from escaping dense urban areas, was found to contribute as much as 0.7&#160;°C of additional nighttime warming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This conclusion underscores the critical consequences of urban heat islands, especially in previously redlined communities driven by both local development and broader atmospheric processes.
</p><p>In response to these disparities, some cities have begun to introduce urban greening programs and reflective "cool pavement" initiatives to help mitigate the urban heat island effect in historically disadvantaged communities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These efforts are part of an ongoing push toward not only climate justice but also aims to redress the legacy of redlining, a policy driven by environmental racism.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chief_heat_officers">Chief heat officers</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Chief heat officers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Beginning in the 2020s, a number of cities and local governments began creating Chief Heat Officer positions to organize and manage work counteracting the urban heat island effect. They lead efforts to try to mitigate the increasing effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> by increasing shade, providing for <a href="/wiki/Cooling_center" title="Cooling center">cooling centers</a>, planting trees, and coordinating anti-heat work.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early heat officer positions were created in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miami-Dade_County,_Florida" title="Miami-Dade County, Florida">Miami-Dade County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Freetown" title="Freetown">Freetown</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The initiative to create the positions was organized by the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Council" title="Atlantic Council">Atlantic Council</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Adrienne_Arsht" title="Adrienne Arsht">Adrienne Arsht</a>-<a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a> Resilience Center.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eleni_Myrivili" title="Eleni Myrivili">Eleni Myrivili</a> is the current <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Settlements_Programme" title="United Nations Human Settlements Programme">United Nations Human Settlements Programme's</a> chief heat officer<sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Athens" class="mw-redirect" title="City of Athens">City of Athens</a> Chief Resilience Officer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Examples">Examples</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Examples"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="Globe icon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/60px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" decoding="async" width="48" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/120px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="290" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Urban_heat_island" title="Talk:Urban heat island">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2023</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="United_States">United States</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bill S.4280,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> introduced to the U.S. Senate in 2020, would authorize the National Integrated Heat Health Information System Interagency Committee (NIHHIS) to tackle extreme heat in the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Successful passage of this legislation would fund NIHHIS for five years and would instate a $100 million grant program within NIHHIS to encourage and fund urban heat reduction projects, including those using cools roofs and pavements and those improving <a href="/wiki/Heating,_ventilation,_and_air_conditioning" title="Heating, ventilation, and air conditioning">HVAC</a> systems. As of July 22, 2020 the bill has not moved past introduction to Congress.
</p><p>The city of New York determined that the cooling potential per area was highest for street trees, followed by living roofs, light covered surface, and open space planting. From the standpoint of cost effectiveness, light surfaces, light roofs, and curbside planting have lower costs per temperature reduction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Los_Angeles">Los Angeles</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Los Angeles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A hypothetical "cool communities" program in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> has projected in 1997 that urban temperatures could be reduced by approximately 3&#160;°C (5&#160;°F) after planting ten million trees, reroofing five million homes, and painting one-quarter of the roads at an estimated cost of US$1&#160;billion, giving estimated annual benefits of US$170&#160;million from reduced air-conditioning costs and US$360&#160;million in smog related health savings.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rosenfield97&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenfield97-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a case study of the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Basin" title="Los Angeles Basin">Los Angeles Basin</a> in 1998, simulations showed that even when trees are not strategically placed in these urban heat islands, they can still aid in minimization of pollutants and energy reduction. It is estimated that with this wide-scale implementation, the city of Los Angeles can annually save $100M with most of the savings coming from cool roofs, lighter colored pavement, and the planting of trees. With a citywide implementation, added benefits from the lowering smog-level would result in at least one billion dollars of saving per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rosenfeld98&#95;83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenfeld98-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Los Angeles <a href="/wiki/TreePeople" title="TreePeople">TreePeople</a> is an example of how <a href="/wiki/Tree_planting" title="Tree planting">tree planting</a> can empower a community. TreePeople provides the opportunity for people to come together, build capacity, community pride and the opportunity to collaborate and <a href="/wiki/Social_network" title="Social network">network</a> with each other.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Los Angeles has also begun to implement a Heat Action Plan to address the city's needs at a more granular level than the solutions provided by the state of California. The city uses the LA Equity Index in an effort to ensure that the effects of extreme heat are mitigated in an equitable manner.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Virginia">Virginia</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Virginia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2021, Climate Adaptation Planning Analysis (CAPA) received funding from the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> to conduct Heat Mapping across the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ten areas from <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> – Abington, Arlington, Charlottesville, Farmville, Harrisonburg, Lynchburg, Petersburg, Richmond, Salem, Virginia Beach and Winchester – participated in the heat watch campaign. This campaign consisted of 213 Volunteers brought together by campaign organizers who made 490,423 Heat Measurements across 70 Routes total. After taking measurements throughout the day, equipment and data was sent back to CAPA where it was analyzed using machine learning algorithms. After analysis of the data, CAPA came back together with campaign organizers from each area to discuss potential plans for each town in the future.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="New_York">New York</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: New York"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>New York City implemented its "Cool Neighborhoods NYC" program in 2017 intending to mitigate the effects of extreme urban heat. One of the plan's ambitions was to increase funding for the city's Low-Income Home Energy Assistance Program. Specifically, the plan sought to increase funding for cooling solutions for lower-income families.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="India">India</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: India"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Several cities in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> experience significant urban heat island effects due to rapid urbanization, loss of green cover, and extensive concretization. A report by <i>The Hindu</i> highlights that metropolitan areas like Delhi, Bengaluru, Chennai, Jaipur, Ahmedabad, Mumbai, and Kolkata have seen temperature differences ranging from 1&#160;°C to 6&#160;°C compared to their rural surroundings. These urban heat islands not only increase the local temperatures but also exacerbate the impacts of heatwaves, leading to higher energy consumption for cooling and posing health risks to vulnerable populations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Mumbai">Mumbai</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Urban_heat_island&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Mumbai"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Mumbai, India's financial hub and one of the most densely populated cities globally, is significantly affected by the urban heat island effect. Rapid urbanization, extensive concretization, and loss of green spaces have led to higher temperatures in the city compared to its surroundings. According to a report, Mumbai is projected to spend twice as much as New York City to manage urban heat generated due to concretization. This increased expenditure highlights the severity of the urban heat island effect in Mumbai and its impact on the city's infrastructure and residents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
